#### ↳ Re: VSAM Error Code 04

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2006-11-07T23:10:09+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ln84h.797341$Jn2.508278@fe10.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<urr1l2psaknq4umoae6dqgg88tkqv0b599@4ax.com>`

```
Clark gave this answer (in general), but to be specific,

Change

FD  MASTER01-IN.
 01  MASTER01-RECORD.
     03  MAST01-KEY           PIC  X(19).
     03  FILLER                     PIC (2481).

to

 FD  MASTER01-IN
     Record Varying in size from 1 to 2500.
 01  MASTER01-RECORD.
     03  MAST01-KEY           PIC  X(19).
     03  FILLER                     PIC (2481).

you could add a "depending on" to the new line - pointing to a WS item to "get" 
the size of each record, but it isn't required.

alternatively (as an IBM extension) you could add
  Record Format V

****

When you have no RECORD CONTAINS/VARYING or RECORD FORMAT clause and have only 
one 01-level (with no OCCURS in it), then IBM mainframe COBOL "defaults" to 
fixed rather than variable format.

P.S.  For future reference - if working in an IBM environment (as VSAM implies), 
then you MAY want to think about also using "file status 2" (not to be confused 
with the 2nd byte of the "normal" file status field).  For more information on 
it, see:

 http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/igy3lr31/4.2.13

OTOH,  I usually find the COBOL status values (FS1) to be enough to figure out 
the problem and when I start looking at VSAM return codes and stuff it confuses 
me more than it helps <G>.
```

#### ↳ Re: VSAM Error Code 04

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2006-11-07T18:21:46-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4rcppcFqoho7U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<urr1l2psaknq4umoae6dqgg88tkqv0b599@4ax.com>`

```
I'm assuming that the records vary in length, up to 2500 bytes.

FD  MASTER01-IN
    RECORD VARYING FROM 1 TO 2500 CHARACTERS
    DEPENDING ON MASTER01-LEN.
01  MASTER01-RECORD.
     03  MAST01-KEY           PIC  X(19).
     03  FILLER                     PIC (2481).

WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
77  MASTER01-LEN              PIC 9(5) VALUE ZERO.

After each read MASTER01-LEN will contain the actual length of the record
read.

By the way, as my programming mentor loves to say "All VSAM files are
variable".  That is to say that you cannot define a "fixed length record"
VSAM file.  You can define a file with RECORDS(100, 32000) and happily write
1 byte records to it.  This does not mean that the file has a fixed record
size of 32000 nor does it mean that it has a fixed record size of 1.  It
simply means that each variable length record can be up to 32000 bytes, and
in this case each variable length record is 1 byte long.

The "average record length" is only useful when defining a number of records
rather than the number of tracks or cylinders.

If any of the above is incorrect please blame my mentor.  :-)

My the way, if MASTER01-IN contains only records of *specific* record sizes
then you can "simplify" it with something like:
FD  MASTER01-IN.
01  MASTER01-RECORD.
     03  MAST01-KEY           PIC  X(19).
     03  FILLER                     PIC (2481).
01  MASTER01-REC2    PIC X(100).
01  MASTER01-REC3    PIC X(1000).
01  MASTER01-REC4    PIC X(1600).

This assumes that there's some way to distinguish between the different
record types, such as maybe something in the key.  Without knowing how
you're using it its hard to say more.

Frank


--- 
Frank Swarbrick
Senior Developer/Analyst - Mainframe Applications
FirstBank Data Corporation - Lakewood, CO  USA
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: VSAM Error Code 04

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2006-11-08T12:53:28-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1163019208.226241.89310@e3g2000cwe.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<8b84l29jcj33vnn859qdf1so0fo7lp68rn@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<urr1l2psaknq4umoae6dqgg88tkqv0b599@4ax.com> <4rcppcFqoho7U1@mid.individual.net> <8b84l29jcj33vnn859qdf1so0fo7lp68rn@4ax.com>`

```

SkippyPB wrote:

> Note to Richard:  I didn't try it but I'm guessing that the return
> code of 04 did not put a record into the record area.

The first byte of the file status being zero indicates _successful_
completion. The record will be in the record area.  The '4' indicates
that it cannot tell you how long the record is.  If you had several 01
levels of different lengths or an ODO then it would not need to tell
you the length because it would be based on data items in the record.

If you had DEPENDING ON then it could tell you the length, or more
importantly, you could tell it the length when writing by 01 level,
ODO, or DEPENDING ON.  One or more of those mechanisms should be used.

> I might have
> abended for some other cause had I not checked both bytes and abended
> the read myself.

You must have some way of determining which parts of the records are
valid and which are beyond the data read in, probably some sort of ODO
or perhaps a record type in the key.  Your use of that would prevent
'abended for some other cause', or fail to prevent, regardless of the
'04' status.

> Besides you need to check for 10 to know you are at end of file.

That is irrelevant to (wrongly) testing for "00".

What is wrong with AT END ?
```

---

##### ↳ ↳ Re: File Status 46 abend

- **From:** Clark F Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2006-11-08T13:34:47+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<svm3l2t8fif4md0qftg82elsle8e4asi4l@4ax.com>`

```
On 8 Nov 2006 04:08:06 -0800, "Thomas" <tmanal@gmx.de> wrote:

>
>padair.o.ceallaigh@gmail.com schrieb:
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
>before.
If your program is on an IBM OS390 / z/OS operating system and the JCL
specifies the use of batch local shared resources (SUBSYS=BLSR) and it
fails on the first read, change the JCL to not use BLSR.  This was a
disconnect between the COBOL compiler people and the access method
people because OPEN of a file for sequential read should issue an
implied start.
>
>Thomas
```

#### ↳ Re: File Status 46 abend

- **From:** padair.o.ceallaigh@gmail.com
- **Date:** 2006-11-08T06:23:31-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1162995811.756093.59660@i42g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

- **References:** `<1162986032.514815.303920@f16g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hey there.

Thanks for the help. I got it sorted. It turned out to be reading the
file when it was empty. I had just coded Commits and restarts into the
job and I forgot to initialize the row that stores the data when the
job finishes ok.

This meant when I tried to rerun the job under different test
conditions the job was trying to read 600 records that were not there.

Thanks again for you help.
```

```
Key Types

The following points apply to keys when using Btr2xfh:
```

```
Actually,
   "standard conforming" COBOL *does* require that the Key field be 
"alphanumeric"  (At least the '85 Standard did.  I haven't checked the '02 
Standard).  Of course, this Select/Assign clause has lots of extensions to the 
'85 Standard, so it probably doesn't matter what is "conforming".
```

```
The file system I'm using (it came with Fujitsu's COBOL) converts repeating 
bytes to RLE fields so the system doesn't have to store zillions of (say) 
consecutive spaces.
```

## Thread: NetExpess 3.0 calling MSMQ — 1 msgs

### NetExpess 3.0 calling MSMQ

- **From:** smonchunski@hotmail.com

### Display Line Position

- **From:** jdobem@gmail.com
- **Date:** 2006-11-10T03:22:24-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1163157744.544775.280350@k70g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hello.

We've been using RM Cobol 85 v5 in Linux for a few years with no
problems.

Lately our Linux machine is failing and we need to port the software to
a newer machine.
To gain a few more tools and funcionalities (linux-wise) we've moved
from Red Hat 4 to Red Hat 7.

Anyway, with that background, we've found a problem we cant solve.

The command Display Line Position is not implemented correctly in the
new machine.
It just prints on the first position on the correct line or in the next
line if its used.

The menus dont display correctly because of this ...

Anyone, has any ideas how we can solve this ?

Thx.

Jose
```

```
Jose,

Consult you RM/COBOL Users Guide regarding terminal configuration.  You
have not properly matched the terminal (or terminal emulator) you are
using to the RM/COBOL terminal configuration.  Change the terminal
configuration of RM/COBOL to match your new situation.

Tom Morrison
Director, Customer Support and Technical Services
Liant Software Corporation
```

```
Chris
```

#### ↳ Re: CANCEL Mis-behaving

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2006-11-11T12:38:27+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4rkgrmFrm8e0U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<1163185596.319248.166000@k70g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Chris" <ctaliercio@yahoo.com> wrote in message 
news:1163185596.319248.166000@k70g2000cwa.googlegroups.com...
> Has anyone had this issue:
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Without seeing the code it is difficult to say, but...

It is pretty obvious that CANCEL is cancelling the whole run unit, rather 
than just the program specified.

Check that you have correctly specified the program to be CANCELled in the 
CANCEL statement. If this is dynamic, display the contents of the dataname 
before the CANCEL executes. If it is static, make sure you have the correct 
Program-ID or File name and that it is correctly quoted. It is possible that 
if CANCEL doesn't find what it is supposed to be cancelling, the results may 
be "unpredictable".

Check that Program B is not issuing a STOP RUN rather than an EXIT PROGRAM 
or GOBACK.

Check that any necessary compile options are in place to ensure that Program 
B is not compiled as a main program. If you exit from a main program, your 
run unit will be stopped.

It is very unlikely that you have a bug in CANCEL; far more likely there is 
a program(mer) error... :-)

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: CANCEL Mis-behaving

```
Chris<ctaliercio@yahoo.com> 11/10/06 12:06 PM >>>
>Has anyone had this issue:
>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>Chris

This won't answer your question, but I have a curious question in return. 
What is your reasoning behind performing the CANCEL?  I have only come upon
one occasion where I have had to CANCEL a program.  That was when sometimes,
but not always, the working-storage in the called program had to be
re-initialized.  The program is actually a vendor program written in DOS/VS
COBOL (aka COBOL 74) that used an assembler routine to do dynamic calls
before IBM COBOL supported dynamic calls.  I can't recall the specifics, but
I think you could set a flag when calling the subroutine to tell it whether
or not to "cancel" the called program before it was called again.  Or
something like that.

Anyway, it just seems to me that this is not something that one would run
into often, so I was wondering why you and others have need for this
function.  I don't know of any other languages that officer similar
functionality (though there may well be others.)

Just wondering...

fRANK

--- 
Frank Swarbrick
Senior Developer/Analyst - Mainframe Applications
FirstBank Data Corporation - Lakewood, CO  USA
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: CANCEL Mis-behaving

```
Frank Swarbrick wrote:
> Chris<ctaliercio@yahoo.com> 11/10/06 12:06 PM >>>
> >Has anyone had this issue:
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
> FirstBank Data Corporation - Lakewood, CO  USA

Sometimes I wonder the same thing Frank ...

This is an inheritted application, so I can't explain why some things
are done the way they are.

Personally, I am a much bigger fan of handling variable initialization
within the code as appropriate/necessary as oposed to using a cancel
statement and forcing the called program to "re-load" every time it is
used.

Experience with this app tells me that they were probably doing this to
conserve as much memory as possible. This is a multi-user system, with
about 1700 different modules that can be called. Looks like they made
use of CANCEL as a memory management utility.

That's my best educated guess anyway.

Thanks,
Chris
```

#### ↳ Re: CANCEL Mis-behaving

```
note, you indicated that it was a Micro Focus product on HP.  Does MF provide 
"Animator" on that system?  If so, have you tried stepping thru the actual
```

- **Message-ID:** `<1163524419.476973.95980@h48g2000cwc.googlegroups.com>`

- **References:** `<1163185596.319248.166000@k70g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <Ygc5h.240080$R52.174054@fe07.news.easynews.com>`

```
Bill:

Yes - the animator is how I discovered the issue.

After returning from the call, the current line of execution (the
cancel statement) is highlighted. When I "STEP" on that statement,
Program A exits. But the run unit does not abort (Program A is actually
called by the main application routine) - control is then returned to
the main application.

The coding is fairly straightforward:

SOME COBOL CODE HERE

CALL "PROGRAMB" USING LINKAGE-DATA.
CANCEL "PROGRAMB".

SOME MORE COBOL CODE HERE


Program B is actually a display conversion program that is used
frequently throughout the system. I'm beginning to wonder if somewhere
upstream PROGRAM B was called by another program and that is why the
cancel is acting oddly.

To compound things, this client has the same code running on two
different machines (identical hardware) and on one it works fine. I've
tried simply recompiling the code in order to ensure that things were
in order - and they appear to be.




William M. Klein wrote:

> indicate a programming error OR a problem with your COBOL system.  In another
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
```

#### ↳ Re: CANCEL Mis-behaving

- **From:** Nigel Eaton <nigele@rcav8r.demon.co.uk>
- **Date:** 2006-11-12T00:34:16+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<mJrxto9IwmVFJALZ@rcav8r.demon.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<1163185596.319248.166000@k70g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```
In article <1163185596.319248.166000@k70g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>, 
Chris <ctaliercio@yahoo.com> writes
>Has anyone had this issue:
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>something but am curious if anyone has seen this before?

Chris,

I see you're using MF COBOL, so I'm not sure if this applies, but here 
goes:

In ACUCOBOL-GT, it's possible to code a DECLARATIVES section with a USE 
AT PROGRAM END clause. That section then executes whenever the program 
ends.

Now, if Program B had such a section, and it included a STOP RUN, then 
you'd get the result you're seeing. That's to say, the STOP RUN would 
execute (quite legitimately) when Program A executes the CANCEL.

That's the only way I can see (other than a runtime error) to get the 
result you're seeing.

Best regards,
```

---

<a id="t-879917bc52"></a>
## Thread: Further discussion on "Something has to be maintained" and lack of OO acceptance. — 67 this month / 69 total

_Topics: [`Object-oriented COBOL`](topics.md#oo)_

### Further discussion on "Something has to be maintained" and lack of OO acceptance.

- **From:** Clark F Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2006-11-10T19:14:12+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7ji9l2dgis531g65si0n4s6rk0tegm4bhh@4ax.com>`

```
When I printed Peter Dashwood's response to my posting, it was 7 pages
so I decided to start a new set of postings.  This one will deal with
why I think OO had not caught on in the IBM mainframe world and some
of my background.  I was referring to some of Peter's postings when I
said that they scared me.  I am glad that the development processes
that he uses produce documentation and have more control than I was
inferring from the postings.  Indeed, if I understand some of the
descriptions of integrated development environments, the non-mainframe
world has available to it better repository tools and means of both
creating and storing documentation than does much of the IBM mainframe
world.  I will let those in the Unisys arena speak for what is
commonly used there.

I only have the version 1 fears of new things both from experience and
hearing horror tales including an IBM speaker describe why Data
Facility/Extended Function catalog handling code had been removed from
distribution.  Even a couple of years later I ran into noticeable
problems when implementing what was by then supposed to be stable.  OO
came out in the 1990's so it is now a known entity and thus would not
scare me but it still confuses me. What is especially frustrating is
that OO is providing much of what we of the SHARE/Guide (or
Guide/SHARE) Language Futures Task Force saw for the future including
checking at interfaces, use of repositories for storing code and
information about the function of the code and organizational rules.
We were interested in late binding and parameter checking so that
caller and called had the same expectations although the systems
programmer in me was more concerned about the costs than some of the
other participants.  This was in the early 1980's.  We also we
interested in data independence and were impressed with the then new
SQL.

I also remember in the 1980's a product called Gamma that was supposed
to revolutionize application development.  The company was headed by
Fran Tarkenton of New York Giants fame.  My shop bought it and I went
to one of the intro sessions.  You were supposed to code up
specifications and data descriptions, etc. and the appropriate code
would be generated.  You could then tweak it.  However it turned out
that there was no good mechanism for regenerating all or part of an
application system.  After much effort, the product quietly
disappeared.  The promise was great.  The concept was seemingly good
but the implementation left much to be desired including lack of
ability to keep the application code at the high level.  Failures like
these both helped those who were working in the field developing
concepts like OO learn what didn't work and added to the skepticism of
management.

Interest in code re-use is not new.  John Ehrman, co-chair of the
Language Futures Task Force and then with the Stanford Linear
Accelerator Center noted that while a large number of programs there
changed frequently, the numerous sub-routines they used remained
unchanged for years.  I suspect that it has been conceptually harder
in the business world where the specifications for such things as what
a sale is, keep changing.  While conservatism and resistance to change
is always there, I believe there have been a number of factors that
have worked against OO moving into at least the IBM mainframe world.

The first and biggest problem for OO on the IBM mainframe is the
installed base.  If OO had been out and viable in the 1970's and maybe
even in the 1980's it might have become the way things are done.  It
wasn't and in the IBM mainframe world CICS and the interfacing
mechanisms used by it became wide spread.  This meant that OO as an
interfacing mechanism somehow had to be inserted into that world.  Of
course, the COBOL support for CICS was interesting and the EXEC
language with preprocessing was a kludgy add-on.  It wasn't until the
late 1980's early 1990's that the IBM COBOL could generate truly
re-enterable code.  This is what is highly desirable for transaction
processing where one module can be executed concurrently by hundreds
of users. Thus a large base of code where transaction modules
interfaced with each other on an input determined basis was built up.
The problems of interfacing were in part solved by use of common
copybooks and other standards.  In addition, the IBM mainframe isn't
the most powerful execution processor going.  The offloading of a lot
of the I/O processing to control units and multiple processor
independent I/O paths partially compensates for this. Another issue is
the z/OS module interface mechanism is more overhead laden than that
of many others. They have addressed this for C/C++ and Java with a new
mechanism called XP link but the COBOL development group has yet to
come on board (heck, they can't understand the need for 64 bit
addressing either).   The IBM support of OO has been desultory on the
mainframe. IBM has withdrawn support for COBOL CORBA interfaces and
come up with incompatible Java versions for COBOL.  The problem is not
that mainframe people don't want to learn, it is that the tools are
just starting to show up in a useful manner that is a part of web
enablement.

Performance and resource requirements slowed the actual acceptance of
OO.  I remember back in the early 1990's reading that an IBM OS2
developer claimed that while OO was very useful, fully implementing it
in the operating system meant that you needed the faster processors
and larger memories that were just becoming available.  This was to
stay even.  The world has moved on drastically since then and the
video cards of today have more memory than the PC's did then.  My
wife's laptop has more memory than did the Irving Oil mainframe did in
the mid 1990's.

The emphasis that IBM is putting on Websphere and web enablement may
well heat up the interest in OO on the IBM mainframe (and the i /
AS400 series).  We will see if they are serious about interfacing JAVA
applications with traditional code.         

In short there were both technical and installed base inhibitors to OO
acceptance.  In many shops, just as OO was becoming mature the
mainframe and / or COBOL and / or the existing systems were put in
maintenance mode while brave new purchased / new tool developed /
silver bullet was going to be implemented.  Also we were going to
replace those ignorant COBOL greybeards with Computer Science grads or
outsourcing or you name it.  

One final thing that I will elaborate on in a separate posting is that
many COBOL programmers came / come from the user side of the business
or were / are on a rotation through departments.  This means that many
of us are not professional programmers and have a noticeably different
orientation than say people with computer science degrees.
```

#### ↳ Re: Further discussion on "Something has to be maintained" and lack of OO acceptance.

```
those tireless salesmen who can sell anybody anything.

When I buy from someone, I like to be fooled into thinking I'm buying
because the product is best - not because the salesman is best.    Of
course if this was true, we would all have different political
leaders, but we like to think we look at the product first.
```

```
"Clark F Morris" <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote in message 
news:7ji9l2dgis531g65si0n4s6rk0tegm4bhh@4ax.com...
> When I printed Peter Dashwood's response to my posting, it was 7 pages
> so I decided to start a new set of postings.

Perhaps some judiciuos snippage might have been better? I now have no 
contextual reference in which to continue this discussion, don't remember 
what I said in response to what, or what you said in response to my 
response... :-)


> This one will deal with
> why I think OO had not caught on in the IBM mainframe world and some
> of my background.  I was referring to some of Peter's postings when I
> said that they scared me.

Yes, most of my posts should be censored...definitely not for children... 
:-)

>I am glad that the development processes
> that he uses produce documentation and have more control than I was
> inferring from the postings.

This is not just about tools, Clark. It s abut management and making sure 
the right procedures and "standard practice" are observed. From a recent 
visit to the US, I noted there is much concern over "documentation" in some 
quarters, but the same people expressing this concern, have no idea of the 
difference between USEFUL documentation and mere pulp consumption. It 
shouldn't take a SOX requirement to make people start thinking about 
documentation.

And there is NO POINT in documentation (SOX or otherwise) that is not kept 
up to date or is difficult to keep up to date, or serves no useful purpose 
to the people using the object being documented.

OO systems are probably easier to document than procedural ones (although I 
agree this arguable). Because of the encapsulation provided by Classes and 
Objects, the Overview and Background documentation of the Class or Object 
can be provided in a Help file and the help file can be a Method of the 
Object. Some OO tools automatically generate current documentation of the 
events, methods and properties in an Object (Object Browsers do this and 
what they report is ALWAYS up to date.)

>Indeed, if I understand some of the
> descriptions of integrated development environments, the non-mainframe
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> commonly used there.

I don't see this as a contest between mainframe and OO... Whatever approach 
you are using, you need to consider what, why, how, and when to document.

>
> I only have the version 1 fears of new things both from experience and
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> problems when implementing what was by then supposed to be stable.

Many people avoid Version 1, but SOMEBODY has to use it, or else there won't 
be a version 2...



> OO
> came out in the 1990's so it is now a known entity and thus would not
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> SQL.

Why would you be frustrated if somebody did what you were advocating?

Because the "wrong people" did it?

Maybe these people arrived independently at the same conclusions your group 
had. The difference is that they backed their postulates with action.

The problem here is not with what was posited, but with the mechanisms 
whereby thought becomes action. (Don't start me on the COBOL standards, but 
that is the nub of my objection to the ANSI process...)

>
> I also remember in the 1980's a product called Gamma that was supposed
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> management.

So, someone has a good idea, tries to implement it, fails, and gives up?

There have been many attempts at such systems, with varying degrees of 
success. Component based design uses this approach. By iteration and 
interaction, a system can be "evolved". This is better than trying to 
generate it in one hit from an English-like specification.

Skepticism of Management is a very fickle thing...

When an idea is first encountered it is rubbished by them (unless it can be 
immediately shown to generate revenue). As soon as a review of it appears in 
a computer magazine, they start to embrace it. If their opposition pick it 
up, they are desperate to have it :-).Within a few months the same people 
who thought the idea was nonsense are propounding how they always believed 
in it...

OO resistance on IBM mainframe sites was not a matter of management 
skepticism. (To be blunt, most managers would have no idea whether it was 
good or not...). The resistance came from the Priests of COBOL, the System 
Programmers, and the innate resistance to change and learning of new 
concepts (takes effort to change your mind about something, or even to keep 
it open... besides, everything I want to do I can do in COBOL) that one 
finds on most IT shop floors.

Why rock the boat? We are secure in our fortress and don't need any new 
ideas creating work for us...

If the mainframe COBOL community had embraced OO, the vendors would have had 
no option but to implement it.

>
> Interest in code re-use is not new.  John Ehrman, co-chair of the
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>
You have my attention... go on...:-)

> The first and biggest problem for OO on the IBM mainframe is the
> installed base.

Yes, that is certainly a factor (sorry, no pun intended).

>If OO had been out and viable in the 1970's and maybe
> even in the 1980's it might have become the way things are done.

It HAS become the way things are done, and mainframe sites were simply left 
behind.

>It
> wasn't and in the IBM mainframe world CICS and the interfacing
> mechanisms used by it became wide spread.  This meant that OO as an
> interfacing mechanism somehow had to be inserted into that world.

Interfacing is a tiny part of OO. The whole paradigm and concept of using 
Classes and Objects would mean a major upheaval and revision of approaches. 
The dinosaurs were just too ponderous to have any hope of doing it.
Instead, it was simply written off as "Modular Programming, 
re-invented...who needs it?"

> Of
> course, the COBOL support for CICS was interesting and the EXEC
> language with preprocessing was a kludgy add-on.

Yet within a year or two of the release of High Level CICS using a COBOL 
preprocessor, the majority of CICS sites had embraced it. As a resuilt, it 
became less kludgy... see point about version 1, above

> It wasn't until the
> late 1980's early 1990's that the IBM COBOL could generate truly
> re-enterable code.

I don't think so. Serial reusable is not re-entrant in the true sense; 
adding RENT to compile options doesn't make code re-entrant. To do that you 
would need to have an interrupt driven architecture that could save and 
restore the system state for any thread at any moment. Context swapping and 
switching would be necessary. While the IBM mainframe has such instructions 
in its instruction set, they are pretty hard to implement in COBOL.

>This is what is highly desirable for transaction
> processing where one module can be executed concurrently by hundreds
> of users.

This is possible using serial reusability under both CICS and what used to 
be IMS-DC. The question is whether they all use ONE COPY of this code, or 
they have their own instances of it... In an OO system, each thread has its 
own instance of it.

> Thus a large base of code where transaction modules
> interfaced with each other on an input determined basis was built up.
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> mainframe.

Odly enough, I don't blame them for this. If they aren't driven by the user 
community (and they haven't been) why would they invest large sums in adding 
functionality to a language that is still stuck in the procedural paradigm 
and which "everybody knows" is staggering on its last legs anyway?

>IBM has withdrawn support for COBOL CORBA interfaces and
> come up with incompatible Java versions for COBOL.  The problem is not
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Discuss the following...

"In a democracy, people do not always get the Government they want, but they 
invariably get the Government they deserve."

(No criticism of IBM or any other mainframe vendor is expressed or implied)

> Performance and resource requirements slowed the actual acceptance of
> OO.  I remember back in the early 1990's reading that an IBM OS2
> developer claimed that while OO was very useful, fully implementing it
> in the operating system meant that you needed the faster processors
> and larger memories that were just becoming available.

ROFL!

EVERY developer has said that, since I started programming in 1965 :-)

> This was to
> stay even.  The world has moved on drastically since then and the
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

The laptop I am writing this on has more main memory than ALL of the 
manframe sites in Auckland (there were about 30 of them) in the 1960s... so?

> The emphasis that IBM is putting on Websphere and web enablement may
> well heat up the interest in OO on the IBM mainframe (and the i /
> AS400 series).  We will see if they are serious about interfacing JAVA
> applications with traditional code.
>

They will be serious about it if it generates money. Actually I like IBM's 
new approach. I think they are on a win with SOA and Websphere is being 
oriented that way.


> In short there were both technical and installed base inhibitors to OO
> acceptance.  In many shops, just as OO was becoming mature the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> outsourcing or you name it.

Some of those ignorant greybeards (I was one) actually realised the value of 
the new approach and embraced it.

I'm writing this on an idyllic beach and won't work again until the Summer 
is over and I actually want to. Perhaps my decision was not a bad one?

There never was (or is) a silver bullet, and anyone who buys a product 
believing it to be so is doomed to disappointment.

>
> One final thing that I will elaborate on in a separate posting is that
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> orientation than say people with computer science degrees.

EVERYBODY has a different orientation from people with computer science 
degrees! :-)

Nerdland is unreal to most people. Fascinating to visit, but you wouldn't 
want to live there... ( a bit like Fry's in California :-))

BOTTOM LINE:

1. The world has embraced OO.
2. The mainframe world hasn't, but this may change.
3. Until it does, the life of the mainframe will be batch processing and 
procedural programming. CICS systems will continue to do transaction 
processing, making much noise about "robustness" and "stability" and other 
flim flam, until they are eventually absorbed into the network and replaced 
by server farms.

There is no point weeping about it; COBOL missed the boat.

I'm currently converting to C#. If someone had told me I'd be doing this a 
couple of years ago, I would have laughed.

It is a wondrous and diverse world we inhabit.

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Further discussion on "Something has to be maintained" and lack of OO acceptance.

- **From:** "Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk>

```
experimented in software development or tools, we just carried on as we
had done before. As organizations we were able to implement change in
other departments but when it came to IT then every expense was spared.

still had to share one green screen amongst 4 developers (with a batch
```

```
topic.
On Sat, 11 Nov 2006 13:53:19 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>
>"Clark F Morris" <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote in message 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>became less kludgy... see point about version 1, above

It still is kludgy and should for the most part have been unnecessary.
By having a compile option that could specify environment, I think
standard COBOL verbs could have been used for most of the EXEC CICS
statements.  This would have been especially true if they had pushed
the 199x now 2002 extensions earlier.  
>
>> It wasn't until the
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>in its instruction set, they are pretty hard to implement in COBOL.

CICS and the operating system take care of the interrupt handling
needed.  VS COBOL II was the first compiler to generate reentrant code
that could be put in a write protected area of memory.  IBM had some
kind of kludge so that COBOL VS modules could be used in CICS.  With
VS COBOL II and its successors there is a fair amount of code at
program entry to obtain working storage and initialize it. 
>
>>This is what is highly desirable for transaction
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>own instance of it.

I would assume that in both OO and non-OO, each thread would have its
own variable storage but could share instruction storage if the module
is coded in a reenterable manner.  See the discussion on ibm-main
regarding reenterable and re-entrant for the hair splitting.
>
>> Thus a large base of code where transaction modules
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>and which "everybody knows" is staggering on its last legs anyway?

Leaving aside the OO discussion, I don't think that IBM has come to
terms with the implications of 64 bit and the idea that there is
inter-language communication despite Language Environment.  I hope
Websphere addresses the need for a development environment such as the
one you have described.  Currently what I see is the OO syntax without
the OO development support.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: CICS and COBOL reentrancy was Re: Further discussion on "Something has to be maintained" and lack of OO acceptance.

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2006-11-15T12:07:24+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4rv0haFtbkibU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<7ji9l2dgis531g65si0n4s6rk0tegm4bhh@4ax.com> <4rkl82Frnap7U1@mid.individual.net> <mr9kl2pq64jqq6um7fvh3hob14fjs6ugul@4ax.com>`

```

"Clark F Morris" <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote in message 
news:mr9kl2pq64jqq6um7fvh3hob14fjs6ugul@4ax.com...
> This will be snipped to deal only with the CICS and COBOL re-entrancy
> topic.
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> It still is kludgy and should for the most part have been unnecessary.

Are you saying it is MORE kludgy to use the CICS preprocessor with COBOL 
than it was to set up all the parameters and call the individual routines 
(as we did before the "Macro" version became available?)

> By having a compile option that could specify environment, I think
> standard COBOL verbs could have been used for most of the EXEC CICS
> statements.

Not without major non-standard extensions to the language.

You could posit the same argumernt for SQL...(and people here have).


> This would have been especially true if they had pushed
> the 199x now 2002 extensions earlier.
>>

OK, supposing they had... How would this have extended COBOL's useful life, 
if at all?

Even CICS has an expiry date... (I know, shock, horror, heresy...:-) It's 
time is limited by the fact that better alternatives for transaction 
processing are already becoming available, and it is locked in to a 
procedural paradigm.

The real nub of our discussion regarding re-entrancy and serial reuse is a 
manifestation of this. CICS interfaces to serial processes.  OO processes 
are not serial; they are event driven.

I believe that, given the same network source for transactions, with 
thousands of terminals all simultaneously vying for attention, the most 
well-designed CICS application, running on the top end commercially 
available mainframe, will not achieve the throughput that a networked server 
farm with possibly several hundred processors available to it, fully 
re-entrant and interrupt driven, can.

I believe this because it just makes sense that the more available copies of 
code and dedicated processors there are, the better the throughput should 
be. (Given that load levelling and message switching overheads are minimal, 
of course...) It is parallell versus serial processing.

At the moment it is pretty even, but give it a few years and the network 
will surpass the monolith.


>>> It wasn't until the
>>> late 1980's early 1990's that the IBM COBOL could generate truly
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> needed.

A truly re-entrant application decides when it is interruptible and when it 
isn't. This would require issuing priveleged instructions in the IBM 
mainframe world and COBOL (last time I looked) doesn't do that.

Please note that CICS doing this on behalf of the application, is a long way 
removed from the application doing it for itself. CICS will simply let the 
code execute until it hits a dequeue boundary; that is not the same as the 
application deciding when it can share resources safely.

CICS and the OS may well manage dequeing for a CICS COBOL application that 
is serially reusable, I don't believe they do so for a truly re-entrant 
application. If your CICS application was written in Assembler, then it 
would be possible, albeit "dangerous"...ANYTHING that takes control away 
from what is perceived to be a CICS function is "dangerous"...

It may be "hair-splitting" to you, but to those of us who coded truly 
re-entrant routines in Assembler and mourned the passing of them when COBOL 
came along, it deserves to be noted :-)


>VS COBOL II was the first compiler to generate reentrant code
> that could be put in a write protected area of memory.  IBM had some
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> regarding reenterable and re-entrant for the hair splitting.

I've said all I want to about re-entrance... :-)

Your assumption MAY be false; an OO Class generates new instances of itself 
via the NEW method. It would be unlikely, in my opinion, that the 
(procedural) code for a given instance is shared with all other instances, 
as the task of managing this would be enormous. Garbage collection when one 
instance destroys itself would be a major issue, just for openers... then 
there are the problems of thread concurrency and multiple state management. 
Furthermore, the code would become a bottleneck for all the threads that 
used it.

Still, as we are both assuming, it is difficult to draw a conclusion. :-)

>>
>>> Thus a large base of code where transaction modules
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
>>>
I am no longer holding my breath to see OO on the mainframe. Websphere and 
AS400 will implement it through Java as IBM currently intend, and I think 
the move in this area towards platform independent SOAs is a very good one.

Pete.
```

```
"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:4rv0haFtbkibU1@mid.individual.net...
>
<snip>

>> It still is kludgy and should for the most part have been unnecessary.
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Most (not all) EXEC CICS "commands" could be implemented via (2002 Standard) 
native syntax.  Certainly ALL CICS I/O statements could be - and with the '02 
Standard including "ACCEPT/DISPLAY/SCREEN Section" most CICS terminal I/O could 
be as well. The '02 Standard even provides for native GETMAIN/FREEMAIN and 
pointer processing.  '02 Standard exception handling would map pretty easily to 
CICS HANDLE CONDITION and comparable exception support.

There certainly ARE things that don't have STANDARD "native' COBOL syntax such 
as:

  - Asynchronous processing
 - XCTL vs LINK (as opposed to COBOL CALL)
 - probably LOTS more

Having said that, I don't find the EXEC CICS syntax all that "kludgy" and IBM 
has never heard significant user demand for using native syntax.  (There 
actually was a GUIDE and a SHARE requirement for such, but these didn't receive 
significant user support.)
```

- **From:** Clark F Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2006-11-15T18:04:59+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gmbml2pnii5enmqfqg8rpgic0l68i6871k@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<7ji9l2dgis531g65si0n4s6rk0tegm4bhh@4ax.com> <4rkl82Frnap7U1@mid.individual.net> <mr9kl2pq64jqq6um7fvh3hob14fjs6ugul@4ax.com> <4rv0haFtbkibU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Wed, 15 Nov 2006 12:07:24 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>
>"Clark F Morris" <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote in message 
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>(as we did before the "Macro" version became available?)

No, even though Macro was before I had exposure to CICS, I can well
accept and belief that COMMAND level was a vast improvement.
>
>> By having a compile option that could specify environment, I think
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>Not without major non-standard extensions to the language.

Based on Bill's posting, I have to concede the point especially since
the major extensions didn't come into the COBOL standard until well
after CICS became dominant in the mainframe environment.  Whatever our
disagreements, I am firmly with you on the disservice that the
standardization process has done to the COBOL community.
>
>You could posit the same argumernt for SQL...(and people here have).
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
>
I suspect that this will come as a surprise to major banks.  They run
multiple application regions on multiple multiprocessor systems.  Most
application processing for little things like online banking is done
by CICS or IMS.  Even back in the 1980's there were terminal owning
regions, file owning regions and application regions.  A given
transaction might be executable in multiple regions.  The locks on the
files were probably a greater bottleneck and now that processors have
become fast enough and memory vast enough at least some of the
processing has moved to DB2 from VSAM or IMS.  IMS still can be a very
fast database for some purposes.  Having several hundred processors is
not necessarily goodness from a power consumption point of view. While
a transaction does not normally set interruptability points, any EXEC
of a CICS service probably is an implied point of interruptability and
for most purposes that is good enough.  Remember that the 3270 is
basically a dumb web page with a submit option.  It is not a Dec
terminal polled character by character.  Many of the transactions I
have worked with in a maintenance mode have had to know how they were
entered, including whether the 3270 (real or emulated) had interrupted
processing.  

>I believe this because it just makes sense that the more available copies of 
>code and dedicated processors there are, the better the throughput should 
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
>application deciding when it can share resources safely.

The application does enqueue and dequeue on the resources it needs
exclusive control over (files, data base rows or views, etc.).  CICS
used to be a single Task Control Block for each region system but that
is no longer the case.  The application does not need control over CPU
allocation for consistency and can be interrupted for higher priority
tasks.  Of course someone had better be concerned about deadly
embraces because of need for exclusive access to customer and product
data, etc.  I doubt the problem goes away with OO.
>
>CICS and the OS may well manage dequeing for a CICS COBOL application that 
…[40 more quoted lines elided]…
>used it.

The code is not a bottleneck.  Each instance gets its own variable
storage (Working storage, intermediate work areas, paragraph entry
exit address, etc.  CICS keeps a use count and for each instance, all
space is reclaimed on exit in CICS even if the transaction abnormally
terminates.  Similar things happen in batch and TSO.  I would assume
that Websphere would have similar capabilities.  Thread concurrency is
handled by CICS or the operating system as appropriate.  I might add
that in this case, I am not assuming what the operating system does
but rather drawing on what I have learned OJT and at SHARE sessions. I
also was a systems programmer so had to get into the technogrunge of
the meaning of reentrancy.  Incidentally the issue of various flavors
of reentrancy and its nuances was a hot topic of discussion on the
ibm-main listserv.  
>
>Still, as we are both assuming, it is difficult to draw a conclusion. :-)
…[33 more quoted lines elided]…
>the move in this area towards platform independent SOAs is a very good one.

Agreed.  Despite my disagreements above, I am not optimistic about the
future of either the mainframe or COBOL because I don't think that IBM
understands what is truly needed for either to have a long term
future.  Massive upgrade and removal of limitations is needed.  8
characters for a program name is a joke.  There are still components
that have to execute in the 16 megabyte size address space and have
all data within that space.  DB2 has a 64 bit flavor and I think Java
does but no one understands why COBOL needs to have a 64 bit flavor to
interoperate well.  Disk drive geometry is absurd (allocation is in
tracks, cylinders and blocks that have no relation to 4K pages).  IBM
has dithered and put out poorly supported and received upgrades to the
application development environment.  They are grafting much of the
mainframe functionality into the AIX and OS400 environments so despite
protestations to the contrary, long term the IBM mainframe (z series)
may be just a cash cow until it is migrated from.   
>
>Pete. 
>
```

```
An interesting response, Clark.

Some comments below.

"Clark F Morris" <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote in message 
news:gmbml2pnii5enmqfqg8rpgic0l68i6871k@4ax.com...
> On Wed, 15 Nov 2006 12:07:24 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
>>
<snip>

>>>>Yet within a year or two of the release of High Level CICS using a COBOL
>>>>preprocessor, the majority of CICS sites had embraced it. As a resuilt, 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>>
OK, so where did "kludgy" come from :-)

I think Command level is pretty good; at least as good as using embedded 
SQL. Both these facilities interface well to COBOL via their respective 
preprocessors, in my opinion.


>>> By having a compile option that could specify environment, I think
>>> standard COBOL verbs could have been used for most of the EXEC CICS
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> standardization process has done to the COBOL community.

Don't start me on that :-) Thank you for revising your position on 
extensions.

I think there is more to this than simply using extensions, and I understand 
why you would like to see these functions integrated into COBOL. Many people 
feel the same way.

However, I don't ascribe to this and would much prefer to see COBOL 
interfacing easily to ANYTHING, than to see it try and accommodate 
EVERYTHING internally...

The current nonsense where a huge amount of effort is going into providing 
COBOL support for XML, is a case in point. I use XML with COBOL all the 
time, without problem. I don't NEED to have COBOL verbs that offer this; I 
can use the standard facilities for XML parsing that are provided on my 
platform. (Windows support for XML is very good, but it is changing and 
evolving.) Would you want to try and build a compiler interface to a product 
that is not stable yet?

<snip>>>
>>Even CICS has an expiry date... (I know, shock, horror, heresy...:-) It's
>>time is limited by the fact that better alternatives for transaction
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> I suspect that this will come as a surprise to major banks.

Oh Frank, spare me this old chestnut... :-)

It is trotted out here regularly. At least one major bank in our part of the 
world has already migrated OFF the mainframe COBOL/CICS to a networked 
approach. I know of another that is currently doing it and I have heard 
about three more who are looking at it.

Networking will not come as a surprise to any of the major banks. The reason 
they are not rushing to do it is because they can afford not to, they are 
conservative by nature, and they are VERY concerned about security and the 
perils of migration. It isn't because of any great faith in their antiquated 
systems. Many  of them seem to be coming to the conclusion that movement to 
a network will be inevitable. It isn't IF, it is WHEN...

(There is a rising crop of computer science grads who will force the issue, 
anyway... Banks can afford to grab the best and brightest. These folks are 
not going to be satisfied with a centralised mainframe approach.)

> They run
> multiple application regions on multiple multiprocessor systems.  Most
> application processing for little things like online banking is done
> by CICS or IMS.  Even back in the 1980's there were terminal owning
> regions, file owning regions and application regions.

Yes, I remember when terminals were invented and we ran dedicated partitions 
for them (32K in foreground 1... :-))


> A given
> transaction might be executable in multiple regions.  The locks on the
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> fast database for some purposes.

I don't disagree with what you are saying here. Time will tell.



> Having several hundred processors is
> not necessarily goodness from a power consumption point of view.

Agreed. A fair point. An one that is becoming increasingly important.

In passing I must note that I am writing this on a machine that has more 
power and resources than the last three notebooks I have bought, all put 
together, yet it consumes a fraction of the power. (In fact the fans have 
never come on since I've owned it...)


>While
> a transaction does not normally set interruptability points, any EXEC
> of a CICS service probably is an implied point of interruptability and
> for most purposes that is good enough.  Remember that the 3270 is
> basically a dumb web page with a submit option.

Depends on how you use it...

I spent a lot of time programming 3270s for use with IBM 3790 systems, using 
macro Assembler. I'm also familiar with DIF/DOF and MID/MOD macro 
generation; I know what 3270s can and cannot do. You can use field-by-field 
processing with a 3270; it's just that most sites don't.

For example, IBM will tell you that you cannot refresh a 3270 in 
conversational mode.

I did it for a Money Market dealer in Spain where the head dealer needed to 
have his screen refreshed whenever one of the other dealers closed a deal. 
IBM Spain came to look at it, muttered about "non-standard 3270 code", shook 
their heads and predicted dire consequences... At the end of my presentation 
(where I showed that there was NO non-standard code, and every facility 
being used was available on the 3270 as standard) there were sighs and 
"Well, I suppose if you do it like THAT..."...

>It is not a Dec
> terminal polled character by character.  Many of the transactions I
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>>
<snip>
>>> I would assume that in both OO and non-OO, each thread would have its
>>> own variable storage but could share instruction storage if the module
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> The code is not a bottleneck.

OK, given there is ONE copy of it and several thousand threads all wanting 
to use it, how is it NOT a bottleneck?

If you suggest that it may get processed across several processors, then how 
would providing even more processors NOT improve throughput?

>Each instance gets its own variable
> storage (Working storage, intermediate work areas, paragraph entry
> exit address, etc.

Sure, but that is not the problem. It is the EXECUTABLE code that is the 
bottleneck...


> CICS keeps a use count and for each instance, all
> space is reclaimed on exit in CICS even if the transaction abnormally
> terminates.

Each instance of what? You are saying there is only ONE instance of 
executable, with many instances of data.

In an OO system there would be multiple instances of both, and on a 
networked system each of these instances would run on a processor.

Although there is only one copy of a Class, it instantiates new instances of 
itself when asked to.

>Similar things happen in batch and TSO.  I would assume
> that Websphere would have similar capabilities.  Thread concurrency is
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> ibm-main listserv.

I'm not questioning your credentials, Clark, just asking for clarification 
of some of the statements you made. I was also a systems programmer in the 
IBM environment (and several other environments). I accept this was a long 
time ago and things have changed, but I don't believe the fundamentals have.

>>
>>Still, as we are both assuming, it is difficult to draw a conclusion. :-)

OK, you're not assuming; I certainly am... :-)

<snip>
>>>>>
>>I am no longer holding my breath to see OO on the mainframe. Websphere and
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> future.  Massive upgrade and removal of limitations is needed.

Certainly. But I believe it is a lot more than that. It isn't just about 
hardware and software, it is about attitude. You can buy equipment and 
software, but you can't buy attitude. As long as people remain smugly 
satisfied with what they are doing, close their minds to new approaches or 
write them off as simply somethig old re-invented, lack imagination and 
vision, resist change, there is little hope.

Even assumng the limitations were lifted, if you now just see it as 
unlimited ability to do the same old same old, I don't think much has been 
achieved.

> 8
> characters for a program name is a joke.  There are still components
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> interoperate well.

Continuing my theme above, if COBOL had 64 bit, it would only be a matter of 
time before 128 bit was required :-) The problem is not about the 
hardware/software; it is about the lack of vision. The world has changed; 
new approaches to software development have evolved, serial procedural 
processing, using the Von Neumann model, is being superseded by more 
imaginative non-procedural approaches that distribute capability all around 
the planet. The answer is not to increase the power available to the old 
model, but to replace it with something better, as has always been the case 
in evolution.

> Disk drive geometry is absurd (allocation is in
> tracks, cylinders and blocks that have no relation to 4K pages).

You could argue that the system page size should reflect the paging device 
architecture... :-) There is no reason why it couldn't vary depending on 
devices...there is no reason why the system couldn't detect the device and 
adjust accordingly...

A much better question would be why it is that the system can't allocate 
space for itself; provide an extent upon connection, expand it if needed, 
release what is unused at close of job, all automatically.

(CDC Cyber systems running SCOPE (System Control of Operations) did this... 
in fact, they could distribute your file across a number of drives to ensure 
that the next available disk head on any device would write the next block 
of your data. It was VERY fast, required no complex JCL parameters, and 
SCOPE always new exactly where your data was. Only if you needed to 
transport your data did you need to force it to be written to a single pack; 
most of the time you neither knew nor cared where your files were... they 
were retrieved by the system as and when they were required.)

It is staggering to me that after all these decades IBM still require humans 
to manage disk space. The system is ideally (and best) placed to do this.

>IBM
> has dithered and put out poorly supported and received upgrades to the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>>

I think you're right :-)

Pete.
```

```


Pete Dashwood wrote:

> 
> "Clark F Morris" <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote in message 
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> itself when asked to.

I support a CICS system that has ten long-running conversational tasks 
(transaction ID's) each executing the same re-entrant CICS program. 
Only one copy of the executable program is loaded in memory.  Each 
CICS "task" (actually transaction ID, more like a thread) has its own 
unique copy of working-storage for that program.  So I am saying there 
is only one instance of the executable code, but multiple instances of 
data, one per CICS transaction ID.  If one transaction ID terminates, 
that instance of working-storage data is automatically freed.

The use count, in my experience, is basically a way of controlling for 
dynamic installs of a program.  You cannot NEWCOPY or PHASEIN a 
program until its use count is zero (meaning no task is currently 
executing that program).  The PPT entry has a parameter that defines 
whether or not a program is RESIDENT, which simply means whether or 
not it can be paged out of memory.  A RESIDENT program cannot be paged 
out without administrator intervention.  A non-resident program can be 
paged out when the use count is zero.

> (SNIP)
> It is staggering to me that after all these decades IBM still require humans 
> to manage disk space. The system is ideally (and best) placed to do this.

Well, with SMS (System Managed Storage), the humans do a lot less disk 
management.  SMS takes care of assigning a volume or volumes for a 
file, and can extend a file to alternate volumes.  The DD statement or 
  IDCAMS define still specifies the primary and secondary allocation 
sizes.  But SMS decides what class of volumes to use.
```

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

- **References:** `<7ji9l2dgis531g65si0n4s6rk0tegm4bhh@4ax.com> <4rkl82Frnap7U1@mid.individual.net> <mr9kl2pq64jqq6um7fvh3hob14fjs6ugul@4ax.com> <4rv0haFtbkibU1@mid.individual.net> <gmbml2pnii5enmqfqg8rpgic0l68i6871k@4ax.com> <4s1s3kFtk8j8U1@mid.individual.net> <FUT6h.281639$QZ1.108530@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`

```
Thanks for the clarification, Arnold.

With the exception of the "instance-dependent" copies of WS, what you 
describe is exactly how serial reusability has always been implemented in 
CICS and IMS-DC (I forget what the re-invention name for this is :-))

Thread/terminal-dependent storage has always been required to support serial 
re-entrance.

IMS-DC used the terminal dependent SPA (Scratch Pad Area), CICS used (uses?) 
the DFHCOMMAREA, ICL systems running Driver used "Core Allocatable Buffers" 
(CABS :-)), Honeywell used the Terminal Storage Area (TSA),  and I can't 
remember what CDC and NCR used, but the concepts were identical.When a 
thread enqueued, its piece of terminal-dependent storage was retrieved. This 
usually held a "progress indicator" which told the executable code where to 
resume processing... the code was always entered at the top, but immediately 
branched, based on the progress indicator, which was set immediately before 
dequeuing. Calling this re-entrance was a bit of a liberty but I uiess you 
can argue the same code was being entered again :-). CICS was a little bit 
different in that the next code to execute could be enqueued against the 
terminal, so the "progress indicator" wasn't really necessary. (I have seen 
CICS programs written both ways).

Although we thought this was pretty amazing at the time, (and it works very 
well even today), I am still not persuaded that Object instances running on 
a networked system, each with its own dedicated processor and resources, 
won't be faster... if not this week, certainly in a few years. Time will 
tell.

Thanks for the brief description of SMS, I hadn't seen that before.

Pete.

TOP POST - nothing new below...
```

```
[snip]

>This 
>usually held a "progress indicator" which told the executable code where to 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>CICS programs written both ways).

What you describe here, Mr Dashwood, sounds like what is tested for at the 
top of many CICS programs I've worked with, the standard IF EIBCALEN = 0 
(Execution Interface Block Common Area Length); if this is true then one 
usually Performs/Goes To First-Time-In, otherwise one Performs/Goes To 
Receive-Map.

I'm not sure what, exactly, you are calling 'the same code being entered 
again' but it seems rather obvious that the code - whether the same code 
or an identical copy of it - is processing the same copy of the data 
areas; otherwise how would it be determined if this is the First Time In?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: CICS and COBOL reentrancy was Re: Further discussion on "Something has to be maintained" and lack of OO acceptance.

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2006-11-17T02:01:45+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4s35prFt7q07U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<7ji9l2dgis531g65si0n4s6rk0tegm4bhh@4ax.com> <4s1s3kFtk8j8U1@mid.individual.net> <FUT6h.281639$QZ1.108530@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <4s2r11Ftma0fU1@mid.individual.net> <ejhe49$1ba$1@reader2.panix.com>`

```

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:ejhe49$1ba$1@reader2.panix.com...
> In article <4s2r11Ftma0fU1@mid.individual.net>,
> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> Receive-Map.

Yes (almost... :-)). EIBCALEN indicates whether this is the first time in, 
as you describe. However, as I mentioned, with CICS you really only need to 
know whether it is the first time in, because if it isn't you will  simply 
receive the current map, process it, then repeat the cycle. (Assuming you 
don't XCTL out of the cycle or enqueue a different module when the next map 
is received.) The fact that the Comm area has zero length means that this 
terminal(thread) has not been previously active and a new "conversation " is 
starting with it. The "first time"  code you mentioned would usually display 
a blank format to kick things off. The program then dequeues. CICS often 
uses a module for each format, so the cycle is simple and efficient.

With IMS-DC (and similar systems on other platforms), a single program might 
drive several formats, each of which would represent a "stage" in an ongoing 
conversation. This is a bit like dynamic web pages; the format served up 
depends on the processing options taken by the user on the last screen. A 
series of linked menus would be one example. The processing depends on the 
options selected, but some or all of the options are catered for by the same 
program. It must be able to pick up the processing at the point where it 
left off before dequeuing. It could be servicing several threads, each at a 
different point in the processing. Hence, each thread has a "progress 
indicator" stating where it needs to be re-entered (just like the use of 
EIBCALEN, except it is now a multi-state switch). Typically, the "progress 
indicator"would contain a single digit number; it was rare for a 
conversation to need more than 9 formats... We found GO TO... DEPENDING ON 
... pretty useful in these circumstances... :-)
>
> I'm not sure what, exactly, you are calling 'the same code being entered
> again' but it seems rather obvious that the code - whether the same code
> or an identical copy of it - is processing the same copy of the data
> areas; otherwise how would it be determined if this is the First Time In?

It is processing the same copy of the data areas, for each terminal (thread) 
that is conversing with it.

The "same code" is the memory resident executable. The whole process hinges 
on a thread-dependent data area so that each thread's state can be 
remembered and restored. For CICS, this is the Comm area, For IMS_DC it is 
the SPA, and so on.

I have used "terminal(thread)" above because you could have a single 
threading system that would still service each terminal. (For years we did 
this...). It just means that a given terminal will process until it 
dequeues, at which point the next one comes in with IT's terminal-dependent 
data and processes until IT dequeues... It is not until you have around 16 
terminals that you would even notice any delay. (The delay is all absorbed 
by the time a Human takes to fill in the format and press Enter.) Nowadays, 
multithreading means they can process concurrently.  (Multiprocessing means 
more of them can multithread :-), hence, the good performance we have come 
to expect from mainframe systems generally, and CICS systems in 
particular... The executable doesn't change, and each terminal has its own 
copy of its own data. Hence, the executable code is "serially reusable".

The difference between this and a distributed OO system is that the 
networked system can provide a dedicated processor to an object method 
instance, which will also have its own copy of its data (the object 
properties, which may include database keys or factory data). There is no 
"re-entrancy" as described above because the process is event driven. When 
an event occurs (mouse or key click, but other possibilities also) the code 
to process that particular event is immediately scheduled on an available 
processor. (Note that the use of remote processing through remote procedure 
calls means that if the local system is busy, another one can be immediately 
used. This is called "load levelling" and modern server operating systems 
have it as standard. More processors, more parallel processing...  RPC also 
allows interrupts to be processed remotely from where they occurred. As 
Remote Procedure Calls become more secure and powerful, processors on 
servers where data is physically located can be automatically utilised 
(although the current trend is away from this and towards letting specialist 
subsytems be dedicated to data handling, and other servers be dedicated to 
application code). Processing of a conversation could occur literally all 
around the World, with data flowing and being processed wherever it was 
located or needed.  Databases in different countries can be concurrently 
accessed and the data brought to one or more screens in diverse locations, 
all instantly.The user who intitiated the conversation could easily copy it 
to other users elsewhere (kind of like a conference call), interrupt the 
flow to make enquiries elsewhere or GOOGLE the web, then resume from where 
they left off (shades of TiVo :-)).  Furthermore, you don't need a computer 
or terminal to initiate it; you can do it from your mobile phone or iPod.

It is another dimension in transaction processing.

The current problems with this are bandwidth, processor power, and security 
(this type of processing is often limited to the corporate intranet, for 
these reasons), but all of these are being addressed and improving in leaps 
and bounds.

Which approach will win out, only time will tell, (maybe both will continue 
to flourish for some time yet) but my money is on the network... :-)

Pete.
```

```
<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:ejht02$7a0$1@reader2.panix.com...
> In article <4s35prFt7q07U1@mid.individual.net>,
> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>>>
<snip>>>
>>Yes (almost... :-)). EIBCALEN indicates whether this is the first time in,
>>as you describe. However, as I mentioned, with CICS you really only need 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> [snip]

Yes, of course. I was keeping it simple, in line with the example you 
posted. However the EIBCALEN being zero means there is no current 
conversation in progress. The difference with IMS-DC is that a SPA is 
automatically assigned when the code is activated so there is ALWAYS a 
conversation in progress. It doesn't use a transaction code to activate code 
the way that CICS does. Simply hitting Enter will cause the code to be 
activated and a SPA assigned. (There are other  environmental factors 
associated with this and I'm not trying to cover all of them here... the 
terminal is in conversational mode for example, the format decides what code 
will be activated (DIF/DOF/MID/MOD), and so on. All I'm trying to do here is 
explain the use of "serial reusability" in two popular IBM environments, and 
how that differs from true re-entrancy).

>>With IMS-DC (and similar systems on other platforms), a single program 
>>might
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Certainly, but CICS usually deals with this in a different way. The module 
to be activated can be programmatically enqueued against the specific 
terminal; in other environments (as noted above) the pregenerated format 
decides what will be activated. Different formats for the same conversation 
will activate the same code, which is re-entered as described earlier.

In CICS it is more likely that the CICS code will decide what happens next. 
(Obviously, it is possible to do almost anything you want in either 
environment; I'm sticking to what is considered "good practice". For CICS 
this means a module per format (based on the TRANCODE), for other 
environments it means a program per multi-format conversation.)


> [snip]
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Maybe sometime when I'm in New York, over a couple of beers... :-)

(That's a show people would buy tickets for :-))

>>>
>>> I'm not sure what, exactly, you are calling 'the same code being entered
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>

No problem. Brought back happy memories of a distant time for me... :-)

Truly there is no such thing as "useless information" (there may be 
knowledge you haven't found a use for, yet...).

The grounding I received in these techniques was very applicable and helpful 
(without being ITSA) when it came to dealing with event driven code, 
multiple instances, reusability, re-entrancy, and multi-tasking/processing 
in a networked OO environment.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: CICS and COBOL reentrancy was Re: Further discussion on "Something has to be maintained" and lack of OO acceptance.

_(thread depth: 15)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2006-11-21T23:07:21+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ek00r9$6iq$1@reader2.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<7ji9l2dgis531g65si0n4s6rk0tegm4bhh@4ax.com> <1164111992.247611.291000@h54g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <ejutdj$nbl$1@reader2.panix.com> <1164137174.076167.163430@m73g2000cwd.googlegroups.com>`

```
In article <1164137174.076167.163430@m73g2000cwd.googlegroups.com>,
Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:
>
>docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>I haven't got a clue what you mean, so I'll concede that round.

My apologies for being obscure... 'Goedel-tightening stretch' is a 
reference to Goedel's Incompleteness Theorem (and a pun on the homonymity 
of Goedel and girdle).

Zen is a product of a philosophical system which did not include the 
Aristotelean Principle of Non-Contradiction (loosely put, 'A thing cannot 
both be and not-be the same thing in regards to the same aspect at the 
same time')... and in that logic is a game played by a series of rules 
(Wittgenstein) then to judge the logical game (Zen) by a rule outside of 
its system (Non-Contradiction) might be similar, say, to judging a game of 
Patience (classic Solitaire) by the rules of Whist.

Russell, on the other hand, *is* a product of a system based upon the 
Principle of Non-Contradiction... so to say that a set can be defined to 
include sets which, by definition, are excluded might seem to be a 
violation of that rule... but I'm sure that Greater Minds Than Mine have 
addressed this matter.

DD
```

```
There was an old story - maybe H.G. Wells, where a magic being gives
the wearer of some boots the ability to have his wishes come true -
and has them pass the boots from person to person.    I read this too
many decades ago, so my memory isn't as I would like.    I remember
one guy wanted to pass through a fence, and wished he could get his
head through, knowing that then his body could fit.   But after
getting his head through, failed to wish that his body could fit, or
he could remove his head - he got stuck.    Someone else wished to be
on the moon - where he saw moon folk, but started asphyxiating.

I could see being granted an unknowing wish - and using it to make
that green light on the way into work today.
```

- **Message-ID:** `<jccsm2tqohvd7j724muhfaj4so5o29bepo@4ax.com>`

```
On Thu, 16 Nov 2006 10:14:55 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>In article <FUT6h.281639$QZ1.108530@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>,
>Arnold Trembley  <arnold.trembley@worldnet.att.net> wrote:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>dataset?'

Actually, z/OS allocation is even more arcane.  If your file is going
to go over 5 volumes (tape reels, etc.) on tape or virtual tape you
need to specify a volume count.  Disk allocation may require a volume
count as well as space allocation in either bytes, megabytes,
cylinders or records of some average size.  VSAM has its own
interesting arcanities.  Systems Managed Storage can be used by an
installation to shield the programmers from much of this but a lot of
the confusing parts still are there. 
>
>DD
```

```
Clark F Morris (cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca) writes:
> 
> Actually, z/OS allocation is even more arcane.  If your file is going
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> the confusing parts still are there. 

http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/dfsurdg1/4.3.2?ACTION=MATCHES&REQUEST=lastcc&TYPE=FUZZY&SHELF=DFSSH0G1.bks&DT=20040903102153&CASE=&searchTopic=TOPIC&searchText=TEXT&searchIndex=INDEX&rank=RANK&ScrollTOP=FIRSTHIT#FIRSTHIT

Has an example of multi-volume non-VSAM SMS allocation via IDCAMS which bears
some resemblance to an example I posted on the IMS-L discussion list a few
years back.

https://po.missouri.edu/cgi-bin/wa?A2=ind0207&L=IMS-L&P=R2944&I=-3&X=59FD9913706C54F89B&Y=bo774%40FREENET.CARLETON.CA

For VSAM you can specify * repeatedly in the volume list until you have as
many candidate volumes as you want to have.

You can also do an alter (/) addvol (* ... *) from PDF 3.4 for SMS VSAM
or non-VSAM. DB2 or IMS won't notice the additional volumes until you stop
and start the dataset on IMS or DB2. CICS VSAM may have a similar issue.
```

```
it.  The web enablement is probably one of the best places to start

It has become the way things are done in many environments and maybe
it should be in more but moving to a new environment isn't simple and
requires a management willing to change and invest money.  It also
requires that vendors have the willingness to provide the tools.  I
suspect that the feuding fiefdom mentality of IBM has been as much a
hindrance to moving forward as anything else.  Also great waves that
quietly peter out has been a hallmark of a lot of IBM application area
strategy.  Anyone remember Systems Application Architecture - SAA?  
>
>>It
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>re-invented...who needs it?"

Given that the province of Nova Scotia had budgeted 4 - 5 million
dollars Canadian for a conversion of two areas of accounting to SAP
and the bill now is around 20 million dollars Canadian, most
mainframers have learned to approach major upheavals with fear and
trembling.  In the case of the SAP upgrade, I don't know if the
problem is in the understanding of what the current system does and
the road to get all needed functionality to the new system, failure to
provide for the volume or something else.  When you are running hard
to keep the current area going, it is difficult to get the time and
resources to understand a new paradigm, the ways it can address
current or future needs, and then get a way to move to brave new
world.  An analogy is available in the railroad industry.  The current
coupling mechanism used in North America (and I believe in Australia
and New Zealand) will allow coupling of cars by just pushing them
together.  However it does not also link up the braking system.  I
believe the Russian coupler does this as do couplers that are used on
subways and many commuter electric cars.  However, these solutions are
incompatible with the existing system and no one has figured out a way
to get from current to future without massive disruption of service
and at an affordable cost.   To make any major paradigm shift is a
massive investment and the short term outlook of many organizations
precludes making the up front investment. 
>

probably wasn't pervasive.  The interfacing between command level and
macro level COBOL probably was not that big a problem so a big bang
conversion was not required (anyone who was closer to the scene can
give a much better and maybe contradictory view).

requires memory and CPU cycles.  Something that is an unworkable dog
on a 48 megabyte, 100 MIPS processor can be a valuable tool and the
only way to do things on 20 gigabyte 2000 MIPS processor that costs
far less.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: IBM mainframe and OO was Re: Further discussion on "Something has to be maintained" and lack of OO acceptance.

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
